1401937519|4.0|The first time I heard of Anita Moorjani was last summer when I went to hear motivational speaker Wayne Dyer at a conference in Los Angeles. He began by telling a story of a woman whose life was transformed by a near death experience (\\"NDE\\"). He only spoke for a few minutes before introducing Ms. Moorjani and asking her to share her story herself. She spoke of her life before she was diagnosed with cancer and how she was practically declared dead by doctors, only to come out of it miraculously. I remember being touched by her experiences and being in awe of what transpired, but I didn't follow up with her story when I left that evening.<br /><br />I had nearly forgotten about Ms. Moorjani until recently, when I came upon an opportunity to review her book, Dying To Be Me. I won't bore you with all the turns that led to my coming upon this opportunity, but I will say that I do not believe it was happenstance.<br /><br />I began reading her book and was drawn into it immediately. As a South Asian woman with very similar experiences growing up -the extreme importance placed upon getting married, being in an appropriate career, putting others first while sacrificing oneself -much of what she had to say resonated with me on a personal level.<br /><br />I was especially touched by her transformation after her NDE. Like her, I was raised to believe that nothing comes easy in life, that one has to struggle to achieve. But after her NDE, she no longer believed that life was meant to be difficult. She no longer believed one had to struggle for love. She came to realize that life is a beautiful place and that we are all connected, that we are all love. All of this resonated profoundly with me and I found myself drawn deeper into her world of universality. She tells us:<br /><br />\\"...for me, there was this incredible knowledge of how we're all interconnected and how what I feel affects  the universe, for the Whole is within me. As far as I'm concerned, if I'm happy, the Universe is happy. If I love myself, everyone else will love me, too, and so on.\\"<br /><br />I found her story engaging and thought provoking, and despite it being a bit redundant at times, I couldn't put it down. I admire her strength and tenacity in staying the course when it would have been easier to give in to society's version of who she should be. Ms. Moorjani reminds us that the journey to be true to oneself isn't always an easy one as she shares her difficulties adjusting to her pre-NDE life:<br /><br />\\"I felt as though I couldn't relate to anyone around me -or more accurately, that others couldn't relate to me.  If I thought about going back to work, I couldn't figure out what I wanted to do. Nothing felt right anymore. I felt as though I didn't fit in with the people of this planet and their values. My priorities had changed, and I found that I was no longer interested in working in an office, reporting to anyone, or earning money for its own sake [...] I felt lost...and lonely.\\"<br /><br />Reading this didn't make me sad, but instead made me hopeful. I have spent the last year re-prioritizing my life and in that time I have realized that we don't really need a lot of what we think we need in life, that we don't have to keep up with the Joneses or other people's notions of what our life should look like.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to anyone who wants a peek into how vast our lives truly are. Dying To Be Me isn't about dying at all. It's about living -about living life to the fullest, about smelling the roses, about embracing each experience with an open mind and heart.<br /><br />Please note: As a Hay House book reviewer, I obtained Dying To Be Me as a free review copy from the publisher. Any and all opinions are based solely on my thoughts and experiences.

0375842209|5.0|First of all, The Book Thief is not a light read.  It took me a couple chapters to settle into the writing style.  The writing packs a punch and you really have to read the sentences fully to understand the beauty of them.  But this is a book that deserves your time and attention.  I have honestly never read anything like it.  First of all,  it is such a unique choice to have the story is narrated by Death.  His observations are at once detached and intimate.  He sees the overall picture, but is still affected by individuals.  It really drives home the devastation of World War II on both a large and a personal level.<br /><br />Secondly, the writing style itself is very unique.  Like I mentioned, it can take some getting used to, but there is real beauty in the words.  It shows how powerful words can be.  And again, it is not a book you can get through quickly, but you don't really want to either.<br /><br />Lastly, the characters are very very real.  They have faults.  They fight.  They falter.  But they also love and hope and dream.  There are some disturbing and some very devastating events in The Book Thief.  But ultimately, Liesel is a survivor and finds hope in even her darkest times.  She finds a way to pick up the pieces and live on, and so to do we as readers.<br /><br />As a warning though, the subject matter is pretty heavy.  I think it falls into that category of Young-Adult-But-Not-Really-Young-Adult.  The Book Thief does not shy away from the tough subjects of Nazi Germany, poverty, death, and war.  Some parts left me gutted, I won't lie.  But I am glad I read this book.

0525423273|4.0|I do not want to write a review for Anna and the French Kiss. I do not think I even can write a review for Anna and the French Kiss. Not a proper, professional, eloquent review.    Rather, I want to squeal and keysmash my feelings. Sometimes you find a book that leaves you completely and utterly speechless, and Anna is one of those books.    I had been hearing about Stephanie Perkins's novels for well over a year, and the gushing and raving that were included in every review made me hesitant. I wouldn't call myself a `hipster', per say, but I do tend to gravitate towards books that are less popular, controversial, and even bargain-binned, at times. There's a certain serendipity in finding an unloved book and realizing how much it really deserves to be loved, but after several months of feeling completely out of the loop, I decided to go for it and read a Stephanie Perkins novel, despite their popularity. I was unsure whether or not they were popular for actually being good (a la Harry Potter) or simply because teen girls wanted to pretend they were the main character so they could have her boyfriend (cough Twilight cough). It turns out that Anna and the French Kiss is both.    Anna and the French Kiss stars Anna Oliphant, an aspiring film critic with a bestselling author for a father. Said father decides to send Anna to a boarding school in France, and though she resists at first, Anna soon finds her place in Paris. With a cast of unique characters for friends (Meredith, Rashmi, Josh) and the especially dreamy and original Etienne St. Clair (best friend? boyfriend? first love?), Anna learns what home really means to her.    The first thing I noticed about Anna was that the writing style is beautiful in a very subtle way. The descriptions of Paris were quaint and romantic without being hokey or over-the-top. The characters' voices, too, were extremely original and realistic, and as the book went on, I found myself becoming very attached to the characters. Not only Anna, who is wonderful in her own way, but her friends- specifically Josh- were very likable, and even some of the minor characters, such as Isla, stood out to me distinctly. I was pleasantly surprised at how developed each character was. I felt that they were extremely three dimensional- Anna was a pop-up book where most novels are flat illustrations. It made the book incredibly enjoyable, and I liked that the bond between the characters were realistic as well. I liked that the book showed how relationships take time and effort and communication and often suffer through many misunderstandings. I really liked that Anna and St. Clair were best friends before boyfriend/girlfriend, because too often is the friendship aspect of romantic relationships ignored in chick lit. The characters and the character dynamics in Anna were incredibly refreshing.    Although Stephanie Perkins succeeded in writing interesting characters, she was rather lacking in plot. The book's major twists are predictable. The messages the book sends aren't incredibly progressive. There's a lot of talk about infidelity, and cancer is used as a plot device, and there is teen angst and drama galore. Anna, though she could not be called a Mary Sue because she was well developed and had realistic flaws, did possess some Mary Sue-esque qualities- for example, every boy seems to fall in love with her, and this is something that we see in teen literature far too often. Rarely do normal teen girls have two or three boys pining after them, and it tires me to see this repeated as a plot element over and over again.    Anna and the French Kiss is fun to read and very romantic. I can't help but compare it to First Comes Love, which I reviewed recently as well. First Comes Love is also contemporary romance, but I didn't root for the main characters' relationship at all. Chick lit is never the most exciting, fascinating genre, but Perkins shows that when done right, teen romance can be very fulfilling. Anna is nota perfect book, and it has some very obvious flaws, but it's the kind of book that you'll want to read with a mug of hot chocolate and a snuggly blanket when you're feeling lonely, and it doesn't try to be anything else. For that, I applaud it full-heartedly.    TLDR; Would definitely recommend, to teens who love romance and/or France. :)    More YA book reviews posted at [...]

B0027MJU00|4.0|Much like Flynn's runaway hit Gone Girl, Dark Places is told through alternating chapters that offer Flynn the chance to try out different voices, perspectives, and even time periods. Here, as the title might hint, the story is nightmarishly dark, revolving around the Libby Day, the only survivor of the Day family massacre, in which her entire family was slaughtered by her brother when she was a young girl. As Libby attempts to eke out an adult life while struggling to maintain any sense of normality, happiness, and sanity, she discovers that belief in the guilt of her brother isn't quite as universal as she thought, and she soon finds herself working with a group of obsessive fans to figure out what may have really happened. Meanwhile, in those alternating chapters, Flynn tells the story of the day of the massacre, working her way towards the horrific inevitability while helping the reader to get beyond the story that Libby believed for so long. Like Gone Girl, Dark Places is absolutely impossible to put down; Flynn's pacing is a work of art, and kept me reading chapter after chapter when I knew I had other things that needed to be done. And her characters here are every bit as fascinating as those of Gone Girl, but especially so in the case of Libby Day, a deeply wounded and flawed character who wears her scars poorly and has never managed to let go of the formative trauma of her life. Neither, for that matter, have many of the characters involved in the story, and as Dark Places unwinds, it becomes clear that the Day massacre left a trail of personal devastation far beyond the dead bodies in the house. Dark Places builds to a reveal that doesn't entirely work, even while I admire the premise of it, but it's to the book's credit that a weak answer to the mystery doesn't take away from the gripping read or the tragically flawed characters that fill its pages. Readers who loved Gone Girl won't find Dark Places as flawless as that one, but it's still an intense, horrifying read that makes me glad I've discovered Flynn early in her career - we should have years more greatness to come here.ifying read that makes me glad I've discovered Flynn early in her career - we should have years more greatness to come here.

B0002IQAN6|4.0|I ass-u-me that the SACD reader here is more interested in a review about the SOUND not a review about the band or where I was the first time I heard it...so I try to spare you that whenever possible...unless it's a REALLY good story... :-)<br /><br />But I feel the need to speak out when the Hi Res reissue you paid good money for is NOT the same version that moved you to call it a classic in the first place. I have a few reservations when there is no verbatim version of the original release present on a high dollar repackaging. I love hearing demos, outtakes, alternate versions, etc. when they are EXTRAS, not replacements. As a couple other reviewers have mentioned, this 2 disc set is not faithful to the original vinyl, with a couple of alternate versions from different nights.  I first pointed out this odd trend in altering the \\"facts\\" in several SACD/DVD-A reissues in my review of, of all discs, Bon Jovi's Slippery When Wet; Not the best spot to stake a claim for Artistic Integrity or Purity I'll admit. But on that particular disc they tinkered with the HITS, adding extended intros and endings with the original songs nowhere in sight or in disc.<br /><br />With all that said, this Live at the Filmore reissue is excellent, but it IS different. With the plethora of bootlegs out there of the Allmans, I probably have 3 or 4 different live versions of each song here on other recordings, so that probably has desensitized me to the outrage here, but I totally respect and defend those who think that changing a song here is like putting a drum solo in \\"Back in Black\\" or changing the song order of \\"A Night at the Opera\\". It's blasphemy, especially when there is ample disc space to have it both ways.<br /><br />The \\"sound\\" is overall very good. My system is good enough that I can tell the master tapes themselves get slightly better, then a bit worse, then a bit better-from changing reels I'm guessing- the result of many fast rewindings and tape bleed when the reels were stored \\"tails out\\". It is not nearly as noticable as, say, Billy Joel's The Stranger SACD, but it's there. Not to take away from you buying this for the sound--the worst it is here is still VERY good, better than my orig vinyl and of course any CD version you've ever heard. On my $10k system with Studio Reference Paradigms and Servo Sub I felt the guitars were slightly subdued but not greatly, and to me the bass was in fact the weak link-I felt the need to up the LFE a notch over the flat response setting the room was EQ'd for. The 5.1 mix was tasteful, with no tricks-just making you feel like you were in the middle of the audience. Overall, a 4-star rating-excellent but not epic.

Culture may not explain all of the differences in development but culture may be helpful in understanding how institutions are shaping and developing. B0002KQNL8|3.0|More brains? Not exactly. This light-hearted -- if a bit dim-witted -- sequel to the 1985 cult classic \\"Return of the Living Dead\\" has a hard time fitting the mold it so desperately crams itself into. Like a young child trying on his father's clothing, \\"Return of the Living Dead Part II\\" can't help but come up looking small by comparison. Its attempt at the sort of humor that jived so well in the original falls mostly flat, save for some shock-induced laughter derived from hearing a young child curse so much in one film. It's the sort of thing that happens when you swap the genius of Dan O'Bannon for the guy who directed \\"Meatballs 2.\\"<br /><br />To its credit, the film is host to some pretty gnarly guts and gore, the sort of stuff that would make George Romero proud. It also helps that the cast is mostly likeable, featuring  mildly familiar faces of the time such as Suzanne Snyder, Thom Matthews and James Karen (the latter two returning from the first film as different characters) as well as perfectly able newcomers such as Marsha Dietlein as the prototypical girl-next-door genre-standard and Michael Kenworthy as the foul-mouthed rugrat. The film isn't particularly well-written and kind of meanders around without a point, but is worth following to the end thanks to its cast and characters.<br /><br />In spite of its flaws, \\"Return of the Living Dead Part II\\" is a relatively entertaining affair that is best taken with expectations lowered and tongue planted firmly in cheek. While it's not a patch on the original, it's at least light-years beyond the fourth and fifth installments. Fans of `80s horror will find themselves enjoying it in spite of their better judgment while the rest will leave it for dead.<br /><br />Buyer beware: The current DVD release contains a significantly altered soundtrack for reasons unknown.

B0073XORLU|4.0|I've been listening to the Brick2 CD for 2 weeks straight, daily, over and over, in my car since buying it. I am a very big Tull fan, and have nearly all of their albums, as well as the last 2 Anderson CD's. My favorite Tull albums are Benefit, Agualung, War Child, Thick as a Brick, Songs from the wood, and Roots to Branches. I'm very fond also of most of the other CD's I consider not as great, but worth repeated listening, except for the period from the A album to the one before Roots to Branches, I do like some of the material from that period, but less so. So where do I rank Brick#2? In the pile of very good CD's I like a lot by Tull, but not in the same category as the top ones I ranked as favorites. Brick#2 reminds me the most of Passion Play more so than Brick#1, in the styles played in the music, and the overall tone. The lyrics on Brick#2, though I would rank as among his very best, outstanding. Not only are they clever song lyrics and even poetry, but they cover the ground of recent events very well with the Banker, soldier, the hedonist, and the televangelist. This album is one where you have to read the words while you listen to it, though that is something I assume a Tull lover would do, but it is essential in this album in order to fully appreciate the artistry in this production and what it is all about. The music is quite different from the band Anderson has had for the past few albums, Roots, JTull.com, the Christmas album, and his last 2 solo records that featured an acoustic version of that Tull lineup. Being very fond of the sound of that last Tull configeration, I was initially a little disappointed in the sound of the music which harkens back more to Passion Play and Brick#1, and is not at all like Roots or Tull.com. But after the 3d listening, I began to be riveted by the intricacy of the composition and instrumentation. The harder rocking songs, in particular, while they appear to be much simpler than Brick#1, they are layered with the trademark Tull Brick/Passion Play style riffs playing off each other by the organ, accordian, piano, acoustic guitar, electric guitar, bass, drums, flute, etc in the Tull of that era style that is very captivating to the ears.<br /><br />There is no question that this record, and the hilarous online newspaper, is a complete work of art, and for that reason alone is worth listening to. I would say repeatedly listened to. The composition isn't quite as amazing as Brick#1, but with the clever poetic interludes, has its own offering as a work of art of its own.<br /><br />If you're a big Tull fan, this is a must have album to enjoy. If you're a casual fan that only likes Tull's best albums, this one is worth listening to but you might not be captivated by it. However, if you are a fan of Brick#1 and Passion Play, you should check this out, but don't come to it expecting something on the level of Brick#1. I'm not sure that Tull or Anderson will ever make an album again in that stratosphere of Brick#1. So come to this one open minded to its own artistic statement, especially the new statements in the lyrics. Look at it as its own thing. In a way, calling it Brick#2 can create disappointing expectations. However, I am very happy that Anderson put this together, because it truly is a powerful and poetic statement about our times, and should be heard for that in itself. As to the music, it grew on me, once I let go of how I wanted Brick#2 to sound. I would compare it to Passion Play both in terms of the sound and style, but also in the way how it ranks for me. Where Brick#1 was an amazing classic, Passion Play was very interesting and enjoyable to get into with enjoyed repeated listening, but not on the level of Brick#1. And that is what I where I would rank Brick#2, a very good Tull album, but not among their greatest. And I'm not disappointed, it is a fine album by a genius rock musician and lyricist.

B0014XCMVM|5.0|Flavors of Entanglement is a grand departure from 2005's So-Called Chaos, both musically and lyrically. The sound is experimental in the vein of Supposed Former Infatuation Junkie (referred spiritually in 'Citizen of the Planet'), leaning more towards electronica this time ('Moratorium' just oozing with ambience). Because of this rather uncharted evolution, Alanis produces some of her most unique and quite outstanding tracks. When the prose goes acerbic ('Versions of Violence' , 'Tapes') it goes bass unabashedly. When the prose goes melancholy ('Giggling Again' , 'Torch') it goes ethereal to the n-th degree. These extremes create an aural rollercoaster ride that is refreshing especially for those who think they got Alanis all figured out, and a much welcomed change thanks to producer Guy Sigsworth. In between the sonic spectrum we get a very stripped and emotional 'Not as We' and the slightly pass&eacute; 'Underneath' -- unknown as to why this was chosen as the first single.<br /><br />Alanis' writing is much more coherent this time, with less psychobabble and syntax distrotions. So much so that tracks like 'Straitjacket' come off a little too formulaic and overtly word-processed, the saving grace being its killer dance beat. The story she weaves could not be any more digestible as in the little-too-obvious 'In Praise of the Vulnerable Man,' possibly the weakest track in the album in every aspect (should have been replaced with 'Orchid') and a poror copy of Head Over Feet. Then she ends the album with `Incomplete', no doubt an incarnation of 'Surrendering' from Under Rug Swept, but with a worldlier message. Overall, this chapter of Alanis' music career is a highlight and stands out as one of her best offerings yet, proving that she really is an evolving artist.<br /><br /> 1.\\"Citizen of the Planet\\"  4/5<br /><br /> 2.\\"Underneath\\"  3/5<br /><br /> 3.\\"Straitjacket\\"  4/5<br /><br /> 4.\\"Versions of Violence\\"  5/5<br /><br /> 5.\\"Not as We\\"  5/5<br /><br /> 6.\\"In Praise of the Vulnerable Man\\"  2/5<br /><br /> 7.\\"Moratorium\\"  4/5<br /><br /> 8.\\"Torch\\"  3/5<br /><br /> 9.\\"Giggling Again for No Reason\\"  5/5<br /><br />10.\\"Tapes\\"  5/5<br /><br />11.\\"Incomplete\\"  4/5Vulnerable Man\\"  2/5<br /><br /> 7.\\"Moratorium\\"  4/5<br /><br /> 8.\\"Torch\\"  3/5<br /><br /> 9.\\"Giggling Again for No Reason\\"  5/5<br /><br />10.\\"Tapes\\"  5/5<br /><br />11.\\"Incomplete\\"  4/5

1439192812|1.0|I have read this Dawkins' book in the Black Swan paperback edition (2012), which is not listed here at amazon.com. Because Dave McKean's beautiful illustrations have not changed my view of this book, I would like to post my critical review here.<br /><br />(1) The author recently wrote an Afterword to Lawrence Krauss' book \\"A Universe from Nothing (2012),\\" closing it with the following sentences: \\"If On the Origin of Species was biology's deadliest blow to supernaturalism, we may come to see A Universe from Nothing as the equivalent from cosmology. The title means exactly what it says. And what it says is devastating.\\"<br />I posted my critical review of Krauss' book, giving 1 star, which means that I definitely disagree with Prof. Dawkins' appreciation of the book.<br /><br />(2) I concentrate my review of this book on the last chapter: What is a Miracle?<br />Let me get into one particular episode discussed in chap. 12:<br />On pp. 252 - 253 the author mentions the well-known story in the Bible of Jesus' turning water into wine. He lists \\"three main kinds of possible explanations\\" of this apparently miraculous story. Explanation 1 is \\"It really happened. Water really did turn into wine.\\" And he writes \\"If Explanation 1 were true, it would violate some of the deepest scientific principles we know, for just the same kind of reason we met in the first chapter ...\\" Explanations 2 and 3 are \\"conjuring trick\\" and \\"It is just a story, a piece of fiction, that somebody made up,\\" respectively. And the author concludes: \\"Because it is so very plausible that the story is fiction, we don't need to trouble ourselves to think about conjuring tricks, still less about real miracles that violate the laws of science and overturn everything we know and understand about how the universe works.\\" This is an ordinary explanation by the author for the claimed extraordinary story of Jesus.<br /><br />(3) Now, I have no intention of justifying Jesus' story, but I do intend to stress that almost every time when a skeptic tries to refute an apparently miraculous claim, he/she takes up such cases that he/she could easily refute but never does other cases appearing difficult to refute! This strategy is very much cunning, in trying to persuade laypeople to believe that there could be no extraordinary claims that \\"violate the laws of science and overturn everything we know and understand about how the universe works.\\" Is this author's logic acceptable? I doubt it, because almost every extraordinary claim is destined to violate some of the so-called \\"laws of science.\\"<br /><br />(4) One point should be noted here that the \\"laws of science\\" to be violated in extraordinary claims are usually some of the fundamental laws of physics. All of the known laws of physics have been established through experiments in inanimate material world. The only point at which human scientists are involved in the experiments (designed & redesigned by the scientists) is when establishing an inter-subjectively agreeable reality (i.e., a law of physics) in their scientific papers. While most of the extraordinary claims concern with the supposed mind-matter interaction (e.g., Jesus' mind [consciousness] interacts with the water and transforms it into wine), there is no law of physics for the interaction, except the case of the observer problem in quantum mechanics [i.e., the observer's consciousness or simply observation affects the outcome of physically perceivable reality of a quantum system], which still remains to be solved despite the theory of \\"decoherence\\" (to which I referred in my review of Krauss' book). Hence, probably no wonder if an apparently authentic extraordinary claim violates \\"the laws of science and overturn everything we know and understand about how the universe works.\\" The extraordinary claim may transcend the current \\"laws of science\\"!<br /><br />(5) There really are some cases that could not be scientifically explained for now. Let me list, instead of the author, some unexplained cases in the relatively modern times:<br /><br />(I) Materialization phenomenon in psychical research: Let me quote from Dr. Thelma Moss' book, The Probability of the Impossible (1974:384 - 385): \\"A contemporary holy man in India, Sai Baba, is famous (or notorious) for his miraculous power of materializing objects such as jewels, medallions, and a healing ash (vibuthi). In a recent documentary film, Man and Miracle, Sai Baba -- who looks to be a young man with an Afro hair style -- is shown at his ashram among his disciples, some of whom are Americans. ... Prominent in the film are occasions in which Sai Baba seems to materialize into his hands rings, necklaces, objets d'art, and healing ashes. But watching the film, a skeptic (or even someone willing to believe) can easily dismiss these `materializations' as simple sleight-of-hand tricks which any competent magician could duplicate. Once more we are in a quandary: Are we witnessing genuine siddhi or conjuring tricks?\\"<br />If one applies the author's logic in (3) above to the case of Sai Baba, then the Explanation 2 may be the ordinary answer; no need to trouble ourselves to think about real miracles that violate \\"the laws of science.\\" Hence, the holy man Sai Baba becomes a total charlatan!<br />However, the very laws of science are not capable of conditioning the phenomenon in which Sai Baba's consciousness creates something out of apparent nothing. While the author hails \\"A Universe from Nothing,\\" his logic in (3) rejects Sai Baba's creation of physical objects. It is interesting to note that while the \\"quantum\\" plays an essential role in \\"A Universe from Nothing,\\" the \\"consciousness\\" seems to do the role in Sai Baba's creation. As a matter of fact, \\"consciousness\\" is a mystery in the current science frontier. So is the \\"quantum\\": How come the quantum? (See my review of Krauss' book.)<br /><br />(II) Materialization phenomenon again: Suppose that a lady [a housewife, obviously not a professional magician] in Florida, USA, occasionally, spontaneously produces on her body surface a small patch of thin metal foil, which has a similar composition to a Dutch metal (the primary composition of 84% Cu + 16% Zn, looking light golden color, with an excellent ductility). What does this mean, if the supposition is authentic? The answer is that the very supposition is out of the question (because the supposition violates the laws of science)! Then, how could I propose the supposition? Because the philosopher of science, Prof. Stephen Braude, reported such a peculiar fact, based on his own field research in Florida, in his book, The Golden Leaf Lady (2007), to which I referred in a paper published in the Journal of Scientific Exploration, Vol. 26, No.1, pp. 9 - 42, 2012: A review of Sir William Crookes' papers on psychic force with some additional remarks on psychic phenomena. Braude also refers to the case of Sai Baba in his book. Such peculiar facts are not the only ones but actually there are many reported in the legitimate psychical research since 1882. (That is, the author does not need to go back to the very old stories in the Bible.) What a contradiction between the scientific theory of nucleo-synthesis in cosmology and the peculiar materialization facts! (This part (II) is what I wrote in my review of Krauss' book.)<br /><br />(III) Levitation phenomenon in psychical research: Which the author never mentions. This phenomenon, if authentic, obviously violates the law of gravitation either by Newton or Einstein. There are many levitation events reported in the legitimate psychical research since 1882, which was initiated in the author's own country, Great Britain. Probably, I do not need to refer to any specific case here because they are well known. Some of the readers may know a contemporary case of \\"levitation,\\" to which the author's friend Susan Blackmore refers in her book, \\"Consciousness--An Introduction\\" (2004:396 - 397). She writes: \\"There are photographs of people [of the Transcendental Meditation (TM) organization] apparently floating above their meditation cushion but these could have been taken at the height of the bounce or hop that meditators use when practicing Vedic flying. No outsiders have ever been shown flying.\\" Probably, even skeptical researchers do not deny the possibility of a scientific research on \\"levitation,\\" obtaining cooperation with the TM organization. In the supposed possible research project, researchers should keep in mind the purported levitation anecdote of St. Joseph of Copertino (1603â1662): St. Joseph's clothing did not apparently respond to aerodynamic friction with the ambient air during his flights outdoors, and when flying indoors over the church candles, he failed to disturb the flames.<br />Maybe \\"levitation\\" phenomenon is not a simple matter of antigravity but \\"disentanglement\\" from physical reality.<br /><br />(IV) Regarding the law of conservation of energy (LCE): Which is considered an axiom of physics. It should be noted that the plausibility of the LCE rests on the assumption that our physical dimension is a closed system. My doubt is that \\"Is our physical dimension really closed?\\" I cast the doubt in a paper published in the Journal of Scientific Exploration, Vol. 24, No.1, pp. 5 - 39, 2010: Rebuttal to claimed refutations of Duncan MacDougall's experiment on human weight change at the moment of death. (This discussion is also included in my review of Krauss' book.)<br /><br />(V) Regarding the interaction between the human mind and matter. I discussed this subject in the above-mentioned [in (5) (II)] paper. This paper reviews Crookes' lever experiments with the medium D.D. Home [conducted in 1871], by theoretically simulating the experiments based on Newtonian mechanics. I showed in the simulation that even if a competent magician is permitted to use a trick to realize similar variations in spring force to the one recorded in Crookes' second experiment, very probably, the magician could not realize it because the experimental results (time-dependent variations in spring force) showed a peculiar feature which could not be explained on the basis of Newtonian mechanics, just as the purported peculiar observations in St. Joseph's levitation.<br /><br />(6) Finally I would like to close my review, saying that there are many unexplained physical facts (mysteries), which have been completely ignored or put on the shelf for the advancement of the mainstream science.<br />The author never fails to say an excuse for this in the last page of the book (p. 257): \\"And, until we have found a proper answer to the mystery, it's perfectly OK simply to say, `This is something we don't yet understand, but we're working on it.' Indeed, it is the only honest thing to do.\\"<br />\\"The honest thing to do\\" should be worked on without failure; otherwise the mysteries put unexplained on the shelf might undermine \\"the laws of science and overturn everything we know and understand about how the universe works.\\"<br /><br />And finally again, I do refer to the possible theory of \\"Reincarnation\\" advocated by the late Prof. Ian Stevenson. His theory could be supplemented by the \\"Weighing the Soul\\" experiment mentioned in (5) (IV) above. If Stevenson's claim is verified authentic, it might be a deadliest blow to \\"On the Origin of Species.\\"<br /><br />(Added on 10 October 2013):<br />I would like to add a quotation from a writing of Werner Heisenberg (1901 - 1976), one of the fathers of Quantum Mechanics:<br />\\"The positivists have a simple solution: the world must be divided into that which we can say clearly and the rest, which we had better pass over in silence. But can anyone conceive of a more pointless philosophy, seeing that what we can say clearly amounts to next to nothing? If we omitted all that is unclear, we would probably be left with completely uninteresting and trivial tautologies. (From \\"Truth Dwells in the Deeps,\\" edited by Ken Wilber in the book \\"Quantum Questions [p. 37]\\" (1984).)<br /><br />(Added on 5 November 2013):<br />The author Dawkins indirectly quotes Heisenberg, who is directly quoted by Chandrasekhar, in his edited book \\"The Oxford Book of Modern Science Writing (p. 350, 2008).\\" The author failed to quote the followings of Heisenberg, which are quoted in the above-mentioned book (pp. 69 - 73: If Science Is Conscious of Its Limits ...) edited by Ken Wilber:<br />(1) Accordingly, in the exact sciences the word \\"final\\" obviously means that there are always self-contained, mathematically representable systems of concepts and laws applicable to certain realms of experience, in which realms they are always valid for the entire cosmos and cannot be changed or improved. Obviously, however, we cannot expect these concepts and laws to be suitable for the subsequent description of new realms of experience (p. 71).<br />(2) Heisenberg affirmatively quoted Eddington: \\"We have found that where science has progressed the farthest, the mind has but regained from nature that which the mind has put into nature. We have found a strange footprint on the shores of the unknown. We have devised profound theories, one after another, to account for its origin. At last, we have succeeded in reconstructing the creature that made the footprint. And Lo! It is our own (p. 73).\\"<br /><br />The well-known old story in the Bible of Jesus' turning water into wine or its modern version of materialization phenomenon in the legitimate psychical research, and the mind-matter interaction phenomenon mentioned above are, in spite of their being in very old realms, actually in new realms of experience because of the negligence of mainstream science.

B005LAIHHC|4.0|Sacha Baron Cohen basically has two groups of fans: those who enjoy his gross-out, extreme form of comedy; and those who appreciate the intelligence behind his low-brow humor.  THE DICTATOR is a nice showpiece for this odd blend, as it buries it's thought-provoking ideas behind hard-R humor.<br /><br />The film follows General Aladeen (Cohen), the idiotic, over-the-top dictator of Wadiya.  On a trip to New York, Aladeen's second-in-command (Ben Kingsley) attempts to have the General assassinated.  Aladeen escapes and, beardless, takes up with do-gooder Zoey (Anna Farris), who has no idea who her new friend is.  As he falls in love, Aladeen also attempts to regain control of his country, before it is turned into a democracy.<br /><br />THE DICTATOR isn't Cohen's best effort; it starts off strong, but then meanders.  The jokes get a little old (until a hilarious diatribe in favor of dictatorships at the end; conservatives will be a little pissed at that one), and the story strays as Aladeen (kind of) develops a conscience.  Still, Larry Charles's direction is strong, and Cohen is still the master of below-the-belt satire.  THE DICTATOR lacks the off-the-cuff charm (if that's the right word) of BORAT and BRUNO, but it's still another win for Cohen and company.

145162607X|4.0|The UK edition of Philippa Gregory's latest release has the tagline, \\"The girl who would be queen.\\" Not, \\"The girl whose father would that she were queen.\\" Gregory veers away from the traditional depiction of Anne Neville as meek and mild, a pawn in the political games of her father, Warwick the Kingmaker. Anne begins her narration as a naive eight-year-old growing up in the shadow of her beautiful older sister Isabel. But like most medieval noble daughters, who were often married in their early teens or even before, she has to grow up fast. Warwick wants one of his daughters to be Queen of England - and he doesn't care which one. Fortune's Wheel spins wildly throughout this book, and Anne and Isabel are rarely at the top of it at the same time. Gregory is in her element with the depiction of sisterly rivalry against a background of court intrigue - it's the same recipe that made The Other Boleyn Girl a worldwide bestseller. She is an expert in portraying the claustrophobia of court life: the constant fear and insecurity which were inseparable from rank and power in the turbulent fifteenth century.<br /><br />The Kingmaker's Daughter covers twenty years in the Wars of the Roses from Anne's point of view (first-person, present tense). Although it is the fourth in the Cousins' War series, it can be read as a standalone novel, as Gregory does not assume any knowledge of the period on the part of her readers. As Anne grows to adulthood, she learns more about the world she lives in, and the reader can learn with her. This would make a good introduction to the Wars of the Roses or to historical fiction. Although not aimed specifically at the YA market, with Anne a teenager for much of the book, I felt it was a natural fit for a YA audience.<br /><br />While I enjoyed the characterisation of Anne herself and also of Richard III - he's very far from Shakespeare's villain but no milquetoast either - I felt other aspects of the book were underwritten. Elizabeth Woodville, queen of Edward IV and heroine of The White Queen, is a malign presence throughout this book, but although she functions effectively as an absent influence, I would have liked to read at least one meaty confrontation between her and Anne. Not necessarily an Alexis Colby/Krystle Carrington-style catfight - given the extent of Queen Elizabeth's power Anne would hardly be likely to shove her into a lilypond however much she might want to. But I would have liked to read a long, in-depth conversation between them where both women would put at least some of their cards on the table - or pretend to. Equally, at one point in the book Anne discovers that her husband has been rather less than honest with her about certain legal aspects of their marriage. The information comes as a shock for her and I was disappointed that she never brings it up with him.<br /><br />Notwithstanding, The Kingmaker's Daughter is a fast, entertaining read which should please - and add to - Philippa Gregory's many fans.<br /><br />3.5 stars.
